A. Summary of Program Objectives
2.2    The Program  supports the same objectives as the Mozambican National Health Strategy, as
outlined in the previous chapter and is focused on basic services, addressing urban/rural inequities,
improvement of staff training and institutional development. Being sector-wide, the Program provides
an operational framework for the overall Ministry of Health. In accordance with the sector's policy and
strategy and under the overall coordination of the Ministry's Planning Directorate, MOH teams identified
specific outputs and prepared the various Program components described in this chapter. The Program
will address the following areas:
(a)    resumption of and improvement in the quality of health services provided so that the overall
sector investment strategy yields a higher level of services, as the sector recovers, by:
(i)      rehabilitating and expanding the network of first level health facilities;
(ii)     rehabilitating and restructuring the network of rural hospitals, and rehabilitating
the network of provincial and central hospitals;
(iii)    strengthening laboratory facilities in health centers and hospitals;
(iv)     assuring stability in the provision of medical supplies, pharmaceuticals and
laboratory reagents;
(v)      supporting a national maintenance strategy in the sector; and
(vi)     supporting an institutional nutrition program for severely malnourished children
and for in-patients; and
(vii)    supporting general provincial operations for health;



-22-
(b)    promotion and improvement of sector institutions, support services and sub-national health
management systems, by:
(i)      establishing policies and procedures and expanding storage capacity aimed at
improving the supply system for medical supplies and pharmaceuticals;
(ii)    improving the provincial health management system, in coordination with a
decentralization of functions to the provinces;
(iii)    supporting and strengthening central administration, thus enabling national
implementation of the Program;
(iv)     improving the sector's information system; and
(v)      supporting the development of sector policy, including systematic evaluations of
the sector's program based on comprehensive consultations with ultimate clients;
(c)    development of human resources capacity, by:
(i)      reassessing and implementing a time-slice of the Health Manpower Development
Plan;
(ii)     developing the national capacity for health personnel training;
(iii)    promoting continuing education of health workers; and
(iv)     enhancing university medical and health science training.
B. Detailed Program Description
2.3    To address these objectives, the Program has three principal domains: (a) Health Services
Delivery; (b) Institutional Support; and (c) Human Resources Development.
Domain A. Health Services Delivery (US$ 256.2 million, IDA: US$ 62.8 million)
2.4   In this domain, the Program aims to support and improve health services delivery, primarily in
rural areas, through: (i) (re)construction, rehabilitation, upgrading and maintenance of infrastructures and
equipment in the health sector, which are essential for the provision of services at the first and second
levels of health care; and (ii) stabilization of provision of pharmaceuticals, laboratory reagents, medical
supplies, and food for in-patients. The goal is to improve the health status of the population particularly
in areas where both health indicators and access to health care are below the national averages. This
domain also includes all general provincial operations. (paras. 1.21 to 1.36)
2.5    The two infrastructure development components are: (i) Health Facilities Development and (ii)
Rural Health Development Fund.



-23-
2.6     Health Facilities Development (US$ 81.6 million, IDA: US$ 16.6 million). The Program will
(re)construct, upgrade and rehabilitate health facilities at all levels.9
2.7     The IDA credit will finance: (i) civil works for the rehabilitation of three rural hospitals (Mueda,
Gurue and Buzi) and houses for hospital staff; (ii) medical equipment, kitchen and laundry equipment and
furniture for the rehabilitated units; and (iii) motor vehicles and bicycles. Recurrent costs, including
medical supplies, pharmaceuticals, laboratory reagents, and maintenance for the rehabilitated and
(re)constructed units will also be financed by the credit through national programs.
2.8     The EU also supports major reconstruction works to the network of rural hospitals. Despite major
planned contributions by other donors, a shortage of donor funds is expected for the higher levels of care.
Other donor organizations, including AFDB, DANIDA, FINIDA, NORAD, Italian Cooperation,
UNICEF, WFP and many NGOs, are heavily involved in the construction of basic care facilities.
2.9     Rural Health Development Fund (HDF) (US$ 30.7 million, IDA: US$ 15.2 million). The HDF'
will provide the MOH with a flexible mechanism to construct/rehabilitate approximately 240 first level
health facilities and other directly health-related infrastructures. The objectives of the Rural Health
Development Fund are to improve equity in the distribution of and access to first level health facilities,"
by: (i) creating a flexible funding mechanism to finance the construction of primary health facilities and
other facilities directly linked to health services in the poorest, least-served, rural areas; (ii) encouraging
project-design/implementing capacity at the village level by supporting participatory methodologies and
MOH-community-NGO partnerships; and (iii) contributing to decentralization and capacity building within
the MOH by expanding activity, training, and responsibility at the provincial levels.
2.10   The IDA credit will finance: (i) civil works for the (re)construction of about 240 health centers
and posts, including water supply and sanitation facilities; (ii) about 600 modest houses for health staff;
(iii) medical equipment and furniture for the new and rehabilitated units and for other first level facilities;
and (iv) bicycles and motor bicycles with 2.5 years of spare parts. Recurrent costs, including medical
supplies, pharmaceuticals, laboratory reagents, and maintenance in the (re)constructed units will also be
financed by the credit through national programs. Staffing of these new and rehabilitated facilities will
be achieved through redeployment of health workers currently concentrated in cities and by employment
of limited numbers of new health workers.
2.11    The Norwegian Government will finance approximately 240 sets of solar panels and related cold
chain equipment and lighting. The World Food Program will finance the cost of mobilizing unskilled
community labor with incentive packages consisting of a food ration or a combination of cash and food
commodities, depending on the food supply situation in each specific location. Community participation
may include the clearing of land, ground work and brick manufacturing.
9A significant portion of rural hospital equipment is being procured under the reprogrammed Health and Nutrition Program.
10 The HDF will assist the MOH in playing its role in planning and coordination of the rehabilitation of the network of first
level facilities in the country. The HDF will also provide an effective instrument for further pooling of financial assistance.
1 l The distribution of the moneys of the HDF among the provinces will be based mainly on the number of people per first level
health facility and on the present average catchment area of these facilities in each province.



-24-
2.12   Laboratories Strengthening (US$ 2.6 million. IDA: US$ 2.6 million). The objective is to establish
a well-functioning network of clinical and public health laboratories to respond adequately to the principal
health problems in the country. The component will support the gradual improvement of clinical
laboratory technology and the capacity of bio-medical diagnosis. The component will also support the
(re)equipment of those facilities which have been prioritized by the MOH. Other essential related
activities, such as training and supervision, are financed under other components of the Program.
2.13   The IDA credit will finance: (i) civil works for the rehabilitation of 12 laboratory facilities in first
and second level health units not included in other components of Health Facilities Development; and (ii)
equipment and furniture for the same facilities. Recurrent costs, including laboratory reagents and
maintenance for the rehabilitated facilities, will also be financed through national programs.
2.14   SDC and (for AIDS control) the EU are financing the strengthening of certain public health
laboratory facilities, and SAREC research funds are strengthening National Institute of Health
laboratories.
2.15   Pharmaceuticals and Medical Supplies Program (US$ 25.7 million. IDA: US$ 19.6 million). This
component has as objectives to: (i) provide stability in the procurement and provision of essential drugs
included in the National Drug Formulary, laboratory reagents and medical supplies to the health facilities
and other drug outlets throughout the country; and (ii) provide a venue for donor coordination in this sub-
sector. The Program will provide: (i) a minimum level of the country's needs for these items; and (ii)
financing for the management and operation of the national drug supply.
2.16   The IDA credit will finance: (i) the stabilization of the program by assuring a minimum coverage
of about 10 percent of the national needs in pharmaceuticals, laboratory reagents, materials, and medical
supplies; and (ii) costs incurred in the procurement and distribution of supplies.'2 Other donors will
finance the balance of the minimum needs for pharmaceuticals, estimated at US$ 1.00 per capita, as well
as the balance of needs for medical supplies.
2.17   Donors providing budgetary support through a pooling arrangement for drugs include SDC, EU,
The Netherlands, Italian Cooperation and WHO. Several donors also provide drugs and medical supplies
through tied aid arrangements.
2.18   National Maintenance Program (US$ 4.5 million, IDA: US$ 2.7 million). The Program aims to
strengthen the establishment of a culture in which the maintenance of civil works and hospital equipment
again becomes a priority. The current maintenance policy is moving towards a decentralized mode where
three regional maintenance centers, established within the three central hospitals, are providing services
and supervision to provincial workshops in the neighboring provinces. At present the MOH is, through
these provincial and regional maintenance units, responsible for technical assistance (preventive and
curative maintenance) for four broad categories of items: (i) buildings (including all MOH health service,
administrative and training facilities); (ii) general hospital equipment (e.g., kitchen and laundry
equipment); (iii) medical equipment (e.g., X-ray machines, laboratory equipment, etc.); and (iv) light
preventive maintenance of vehicles.
12 Until the National Drug Formulary is updated, orders of drugs will require explicit approval by the National Director of
Health.



-25-
2.19   The IDA credit will finance: (i) a part of the national needs in tools and shop equipment for the
reinforcement of the national and regional centers and the establishment of provincial units; (ii) partial
needs in spares and materials for the system based on the needs and requests coming from the regional
and provincial levels; (iii) pre-service training, on-the-job-training, seminars, and supervision activities
especially regional supervision of provinces; (iv) vehicles for regional centers and motor bikes for the
provincial units; and (v) technical assistance in hospital maintenance management to be located in the
national maintenance center, to support the supervision and management of the maintenance
decentralization.
2.20   Other donors supporting the National Maintenance Program include The Netherlands and Italian
Cooperation.
2.21   Institutional Feeding Program (US$ 10.4 million). The purpose of this component is to: (i) treat
severely malnourished children; (ii) provide in-patients of hospitals and health centers with adequate food
in order to accelerate patient recovery and thus increase hospital efficiency; and (iii) provide meals for
health workers participating in training courses.
2.22   The Program will finance: (i) food commodities (milk, oil and sugar) for therapeutic feeding of
severely malnourished children; (ii) the costs of providing regular meals to in-patients as well as health
workers attending courses in training institutions. The program will be financed by WFP, EU and SDC
through budgetary support and in-kind participation.
2.23   General Province Operations (US$ 100.8 million, IDA US$ 6.1 million). This component
accounts for all recurrent expenditures at the provincial level not included in other national programs.
The Program will finance part of the non-salary recurrent costs of all province operations. The bulk of
these expenditures will be financed by the GOM through the national budget.
2.24   The IDA credit will finance goods and services (e.g., some medical supplies, inputs for
maintenance of buildings and equipment), which are not being procured centrally through one of the
national programs.
2.25   Other donors supporting the general provincial operations, with TA, materials and supplies, and
budgetary support, include DANIDA, FINIDA, the Italian Cooperation, SDC, WHO and UNICEF.
Domain B. Institutional Support (US$ 63.6 million, IDA: US$ 14.8 million)
2.26   In this domain, the Program  aims to support and strengthen the sector management and
administration capacity through: (i) provision of a framework for continuous and sustainable management
improvements in the health system, with emphasis on the provincial and district levels and on financial
and personnel management; (ii) establishment of policies, procedures and infrastructures for effective
management of supplies; (iii) assistance to GACOPI to improve the administration of projects at central
and provincial levels; (iv) further development and increased coverage of the Health Information System;
and (v) assistance to sector policy development and project preparation. This component also includes the
general central operations. (paras. 1.37 to 1.42)
2.27   The main donors giving institutional support include AFDB, DANIDA, FINIDA, Italian
Cooperation, OPEC Fund, SDC, UNICEF and USAID.



-26-
2.28   Supply System Improvement (US$ 3.5 million, IDA: US$ 2.7 million). This component will help:
(i) establish policies, procedures and infrastructure required at the health unit, district and provincial levels
for effectively matching the supply of health-related commodities, including pharmaceuticals, to needs and
demands; and (ii) improve the efficiency of overall supply operations for all levels of health facilities. In
this context, the Program will support the development of a standardized list of basic medical supplies,
equipment and consumables in common use and of a stock control system at health unit, district, and
provincial levels.
2.29   The IDA credit will finance: (i) the development of a standard list of medical supplies in common
use, the development of a stock control system for use at health unit, district, and provincial levels and
the framing of a stock-holding policy for provincial health directorates, and further training of health
workers related to the distribution system; (ii) civil works for the construction of two warehouses for
medical supplies and pharmaceuticals (Pemba and Lichinga) and the upgrading of the Beira pharmaceutical
warehouse; (iii) equipment for the warehouses constructed and rehabilitated under the credit (forklifts and
pallet racks); and (iv) motor vehicles for the sector distribution chain.
2.30   The civil works financed by IDA complement major contributions of other donors for the
construction of warehouses in Chokwe, Mocuba and Cuamba. UNICEF supports the improvement of the
MOH transportation system.
2.31   Provincial Health Management Improvement (US$ 8.9 million, IDA: US$ 3.0 million). This
component will assist in: (i) identifying management problem areas, especially at the provincial level;
(ii) providing an overall framework and a realistic schedule for continuous and sustainable management
improvement at all levels; and (iii) undertaking actions derived from the problem identification and
framework development phases of the management improvement effort targeted at strengthening
management and administrative capacities within provincial-level health care operations, with special
attention to personnel and financial management systems and to donor coordination.
2.32   The component will operate at several levels over the course of the Program to strengthen
management capacities at the provincial and district levels, to improve task monitoring as well as health
post, health center, rural hospital and provincial hospital administration. The management improvement
program would focus initially on the provincial level and subsequently on district level operations. In
personnel management, the specific objectives are: (i) development of a personnel database at central and
provincial levels to improve the accuracy of personnel data; and (ii) delegation to the provinces of
decision making authority in most matters of staff management. In financial management improvement,
the specific objectives are: (i) strengthening procedures to coordinate the planning and budgeting activities
of the health sector, and development of a financial information system to support decision-making; (ii)
improvement of the financial management procedures to facilitate the coordination of external aid; and
(iii) improvement of the cost recovery mechanisms.
2.33   The IDA credit will finance: (i) the identification of management problem areas, the development
of actions derived from problem identification and the decentralization process of the sector management
with emphasis on personnel management - development of the personnel database, and procedures manual
and training of central and provincial staff - and finance management - improvement of public budget
management and external aid management, development of the sectoral financial information system,
consolidation of the cost recovery mechanisms and development and installation of an accounting plan
adapted for the health units; (ii) computers and other equipment and furniture for central and provincial
levels needed to improve personnel and finance management; (iii) minor civil works -- repairs and



-27-
facilities upgrading -- at the provincial health directorates; (iv) partial needs for office supplies; and (v)
partial needs for supervision and support visits by central and provincial staff, complemented by visits and
in-service training provided through the Human Resources Development component.
2.34   Other donors supporting management improvement at the provincial level include DANIDA,
FINIDA, Italian Cooperation, Netherlands, NORAD, SDC, UNICEF and USAID.
2.35   Information System Improvement (US$ 2.5 million. IDA: US$ 1.3 million). This component
supports the further development and expanded coverage of the Health Information System (HIS), the core
of which has already been developed by the MOH and is presently being implemented for levels I and
11 of the health care system. Under the Program, support will be provided for HIS expansion to include
definition, collection, processing and use of basic epidemiologic and basic hospital efficiency and services
data for Levels III and IV of the health services system. The Program will support the continuation and
expansion of internal management information system improvements initiated under the IDA-financed
Health and Nutrition Project.
2.36   The IDA credit will finance: (i) the development, pilot testing and installation of the information
system for provincial hospitals and strengthening of data collection and processing at levels I and II of
the health system; (ii) computer equipment and furniture for provincial hospitals' statistics units and for
installation of a pilot data transfer system using a computer networking system linking the provincial and
central level; and (iii) goods and services for the running of the health information system over a period
of two and half years.
2.37   DANIDA and USAID also support strengthening of the Health Information System.
2.38   Program Management (US$ 47.6 million. IDA: US$ 6.9 million). A number of activities, such
as those implemented through national programs, are administered at the Ministry (central) level. This
component accounts for the general recurrent costs, including salaries and goods and services incurred
by the central agencies and directorates that administer these programs. The Program will provide
assistance to enable the MOH to improve the administration of investment projects and maintenance
coordination. The component will also assist in the development of policies to enable the MOH to
respond to the many challenges being introduced by peace, skilled manpower shortages, massive
migrations, etc. In addition to funding studies and preliminary policy implementation in areas of
institutional organization, personnel administration, AIDS, in-patient nutrition, hospital waste disposal and
family planning, among others, the Program will also finance monitoring and evaluation as well as the
preparation of new sector projects.
2.39   The IDA credit will finance: (i) technical assistance to strengthen the national directorates'
capacity to implement Program activities; (ii) 10 provincial coordinators, 3 engineers, 12 field engineers,
and 15 basic level technicians for the Provincial Planning Units (NEPs); (iii) vehicles, other equipment
and furniture to equip the NEPs; (iv) part of GACOPI's and the NEPs' operating costs; (v) studies to
support policy development at the MOH; (vi) technical assistance to coordinate all monitoring and
evaluation activities in the sector and to assist in the development of technical capacity within the MOH
to monitor main activities in the sector program and to provide timely evaluation reports for internal use
as well as by donors; and (vii) preparation of tender documents and for activities to be undertaken in
future projects of the MOH.



-28-
2.40   The Program will also finance the recurrent costs of all central operations. The bulk of these
expenditures will be financed by the GOM through the national budget.
2.41   Other donors supporting this component include AFDB, Italian Cooperation, OPEC Fund,
UNICEF and USAID.
Domain C. Human Resources Development (lS$ 35.9 million. IDA: US$ 21.2 million)
2.42   The Ministry of Health's plans for the rehabilitation and further development of Mozambique's
health services recognize that human resources in the sector are a key limiting factor in that development.
The ten-year Health Manpower Development Plan (HMDP) for 1992 through 2002, prepared with support
from the IDA-financed Health and Nutrition Project, was approved in March 1992. The HMDP is based,
in part, on national level health development plans, but primarily on the analysis of current staff
composition and training capacity and on MOH projections through 2002 of funds expected to be available
for MOH staff salaries. HMDP implementation is periodically reviewed in light of growth of the MOH
budget, progress in strengthening of MOH training capacity, MOH management of Human Resources
Development, and the implications of peace and of the resultant increased territorial access and major
population movements. (paras. 1.43 to 1.48)
2.43  In the domain of Human Resources Development, the Program aims to: (i) assist the MOH in the
implementation of the Health Manpower Development Plan; (ii) strengthen the national capacity for health
personnel training; (iii) promote continuing education of health workers; and (iv) provide key support to
the Faculty of Medicine.
2.44   Health Manpower Development Plan Implementation (US$ 17.2 million. IDA: US$ 13.6 million).
This component will assist in the further implementation of the manpower plan by financing a time-slice
of the total activities foreseen in the plan. The MOH training experience, including experience under the
Health and Nutrition Project, indicates a likelihood that training delays will slow execution so that three
years of the plan's courses will be completed during the five years of the Program. Therefore, the
Program over five years will finance those courses which the plan schedules to begin during the years
1996, 1997 and 1998 and to be completed before the end of the year 2000 (a total of 48 courses); each
such course would be financed throughout its entire duration by the credit. In the case that annual
evaluation shows that training system improvements have led to more rapid implementation such as that
foreseen in the original manpower plan and therefore to more rapid expenditure of the Program's training
funds, it is anticipated that other financing will be available to continue the more rapid implementation.
2.45   The IDA credit will finance: (i) the total costs of implementing 48 courses; and (ii) scholarships
covering students' partial subsistence, course-related travel and rural practice activities.
2.46   The main donors involved in this component include AFDB, DANIDA, FINIDA, EU, Italian
Cooperation, SDC, UNICEF and WHO.
2.47   Training Capacity Improvement (US$ 12.3 million, IDA: US$ 4.7 million). This component will
improve the capacity of the MOH to train its staff by financing: (i) upgrading and refurbishing of training
institutions; and (ii) improving the delivery of training itself.
2.48   The IDA credit will finance: (i) civil works for rehabilitation and extension of the training
institutes in Maputo, Beira and Nampula and of training centers in Chicumbane and Chicuque;



-29-
(ii) furniture for the rehabilitated facilities; (iii) training materials and equipment for all health training
institutes and centers; and (iv) motor vehicles. The credit's contribution complements the civil works
activities for rehabilitation of the Training Institutes for mid-level healthworkers in Maputo, Beira and
Nampula and construction/extension of basic training facilities in Cuamba and Lichinga, partially
implemented under the Health and Nutrition Project.
2.49   DANIDA, FINIDA, SDC, EU, WFP and UNICEF are the other donors supporting the MHS
through this component.
2.50   In-service Training Improvement (US$ 2.6 million. IDA: US$ 2.3). This component will support
improvements in and rationalization of continuing education activities to improve the effectiveness and
efficiency of MOH health services and their planning, management and support. The MOH, in
collaboration with donors, will work to rationalize in-service training, eliminate undesirable overlaps and
duplications, focus training on priority needs for provision of basic packages of health services, increase
and monitor training effectiveness, and reduce the training-related losses of service time observed in
recent years.
2.51   The IDA credit will finance: (i) the costs of in-service training courses in the MOH/WHO
Regional Center for Health Development (CRDS) in Maputo; (ii) technical assistance in continuing
education development and management; (iii) the costs of in-service training activities at the provincial
level (after selection among province-submitted proposals on the basis of relevance to priorities, likely
impact, and cost).
2.52 All other donors supporting the MHS through General Training Operations (e.g., UNICEF, WHO,
DANIDA, FINIDA, NORAD, SDC, STC, UNFPA, USAID, UNDP) will continue to finance continuing
education activities. The MOH has initiated discussions of multi-donor pooling of funds to support such
activities, in keeping with the component's objectives.
2.53   Faculty of Medicine Sup2ort (US$ 0.6 million, IDA: US$ 0.6 million). The objective of this
component is to improve the quality of medical training provided at the university, particularly in
rural/community medicine. The Program will assist in the following areas: (i) training and practical
experience for medical students in rural facilities and communities, in cooperation with NGOs;
(ii) provision of texts and reference materials for medical students (including basic reference manuals to
be retained by them after graduation); and (iii) transportation for students engaged in community medicine
training.
2.54   The IDA credit will finance: (i) the cost of textbooks and reference materials for faculty,
professionals and students; (ii) the cost of setting up, running and supervising rural internships including
four staff-months of local technical assistance for an external evaluation of this activity; and (iii) a mini-
bus. Because the Faculty of Medicine is part of the University and funded mainly by and through the
Ministry of Education, no other funding sources are shown under the MOH Program.
2.55   General Training Operations (US$ 3.1 million) This component, supported by the EU, UNICEF,
WHO, NORAD, SDC, UNFPA, USAID, UNDP, DANIDA, FINIDA and the Italian Cooperation
finances general investment and recurrent expenditures in human resources development, outside those
outlined in the components above.
2.56   In addition to taxes on all items financed by IDA, in the Human Resources Development domain,
government will finance salaries of the students in training.

D. Program Financing
Financing Plan
2.58   The Program is designed to finance a five year time-slice of the MHS. Financing for the first
three years of the program is based on firmer commitments, whereas the last two years have only partial
funding to-date.  Alternative scenarios of government commitment to the health sector have been
developed. Under the base case scenario, the government will contribute approximately US$ 116 million,
of which some US$ 98 million in recurrent expenditures. This total corresponds to an average of about
US$ 1.0 per capita annually and is in line with a 4 percent annual increase in the recurrent budget
between 1996-2000, following a sharp initial increase of over 40 percent for 1995. (para. 1.14) An
increasing share of the local contribution will come from cost recovery, particularly in pharmaceuticals.
(Box 2) All major donors operating in the sector, '4in association, have committed to finance, on a parallel
basis, an estimated sum of about US$ 239 million (IDA included) (Annex-2.1A). The amount of IDA
financing, US$ 98.7 million, is based on the government's estimate of sustainable financial commitment.
If the Program is implemented at an accelerated rate, donor support including IDA would be reassessed
with the objective of maintaining commensurate support. If, on the other hand, the Program experiences
implementation delays or if co-funding or counterpart funding is not forthcoming, the Program may be
scaled down at the mid-term review.

2.59    During the life of the Program, donors will progressively shift the focus of finance from specific
projects to the Program, with a parallel shift from appraising individual activities to financing shares of
the whole sector strategy.
Program Sustairnability
2.60    The MOH  explicitly designed the National Health Sector Strategy and Program  to deliver a
cost-effective package of basic health services. The eventual sustainability of services is enhanced by
emphasis on basic rural facilities, with rural hospitals for referral services, appropriate training and use
of mid-level health personnel, redeployment of existing resources, provision of generic essential
medicines, community participation and cost recovery, and strengthened management support and
supervision. Efficiency will also be enhanced by greater budget flexibility introduced by the integrated
sector approach. However, in spite of the government's concurrent commitment to increasing levels of
funding for the sector, foreseeable economic growth in Mozambique indicates that external support will



-32-
be necessary to sustain health and other services for some years after the present time slice financed by
the IDA credit. (paras. 1.12 and 1.13)
2.61   IDA financing of incremental recurrent costs amounts to about US$ 8.5 million (excluding
pharmaceuticals) and represent less than 5 percent of the Programs's total recurrent expenditures
(excluding pharmaceuticals). If we include pharmaceuticals, the share of IDA's recurrent expenditures
financing is still only about 10 percent of the total. The share of IDA in recurrent expenditures declines
over the life of the Program as the government takes on an increasing share of financing of the Program.
(paras. 1.14 and 2.58) IDA financing of recurrent expenditures concentrates on medical supplies, fuel,
maintenance inputs and laboratory reagents (as well as pharmaceuticals).  IDA funds are not being
allocated for salaries of MOH staff. (Annex-2.IA and 2.2B)



-33-
III. PROGRAM IMPLEMENTATION
A. Implementation Arrangements
Program Management Structure
3.1    The MOH, through its line agencies, will have overall responsibility for management of all
Program activities. The Director of the National Directorate of Planning and Cooperation (DNPC), who
reports directly to the Minister of Health, will be responsible for coordinating and supervising Program
implementation. The Director will liaise among the various implementation institutions, the Ministry of
Finance, the Bank of Mozambique and donors. Assisted by a Program Coordinator, (s)he will meet
regularly with implementing agencies and will monitor progress, keep program accounts, and prepare
reports, as required and agreed by the government and donors. To facilitate program coordination and
monitoring, the Minister's Advisory Board (Conselho Consultivo do Ministro), comprising the Minister,
the Vice Minister and heads of all national Directorates and Departments, will meet once every three
months to review progress in all Program activities and to resolve any issues impeding achievement of
Program objectives. The internal organization of the MOH is presented in Annex-1.1.
3.2    As program-supported investments (particularly construction and rehabilitation as in the case of
the Rural Health Development Fund) will be highly concentrated in rural areas and spread over several
locations in each province, each Provincial Directorate of Health should have technical capacity to take
responsibility for management of rehabilitation activities. Therefore, Provincial Construction and
Maintenance Units, which are part of the Provincial Directorate of Health/NEPs, will gradually be
strengthened in all 10 provinces and Maputo City. These Units, which will be responsible for the
day-to-day management and monitoring of construction activities, particularly those related to the Rural
Health Development Fund, will report to the Provincial Director of Health. GACOPI will be in charge
of preparation of norms and standards for the construction and maintenance of infrastructures and of
approval of designs and large contracts.
3.3    GACOPI is the technical unit of the DNPC, in the Central MOH, which is responsible for
administering and supervising all health sector investments involving construction or rehabilitation of
equipment and health-related infrastructures, including the preparation of the sector's investment plans.
GACOPI will also be responsible for the technical coordination of the work of the Provincial Maintenance
and Construction Units to be strengthened to support the Program. For the purposes of the IDA credit,
GACOPI will manage financial accounts and procurement processes (for large civil works, and central
purchases of goods and services). The emphasis is on shifting Program management to lower tiers of
government and toward greater accountability for implementation processes.  The participation of
communities in the design (through systematic client consultation--SCC--methodologies) and execution of
health facilities construction and in other aspects of health services, which is integral to this Program, is
expected to yield greater efficiency and accountability in the provision of services as well as a greater
sense of ownership of the Program. (para. 2.9)
Implementation Agencies
3.4    The DNPC, directly responsible for overall coordination of the Institutional Support components
of the Program, also will be directly responsible for the components for: (a) Provincial Health
Management Improvement; (b) Health Information System Improvement; and (c) Program Management.



-34-
The Supply System Improvement component will be a responsibility of the Supplies Directorate, and the
Financial Management Improvement components will be a responsibility of the Administration and
Finance Directorate.
3.5    The National Directorate of Health will be directly responsible, at the central level, for
implementation of the following components: (a) Health Facilities Development; (b) Rural Health
Development Fund; (c) Laboratories Strengthening; (d) Pharmaceuticals and Medical Supplies Program
Stabilization; (e) Maintenance Program Support; (f) Institutional Feeding Program; and (g) Provincial
Operations.
3.6    The HDF will be set up for the (re)construction of first level facilities and small projects directly
linked to health, targeted to the poorest, least-served, mainly rural areas, as determined by the MOH,
based on Bank-agreed criteria. In order to prevent the construction of first level facilities which are not
integrated in a network of services, new first level facilities will only be built within under-served parts
of the catchment areas of a well functioning second level facility for which health staff is available. The
Fund will finance small-scale, rapidly disbursing projects to be designed with the assistance of a
participatory planning process. The local community which will benefit from the new facility will be
involved in its design, its construction and its maintenance. It is foreseen that a health worker, who is part
of the personnel structure of the MOH, will be posted in the locality to guide the process of consulting
the community on the facility and on services to be delivered. The work of the health worker can
spearhead the process of increasing community organization and capacity, which are essential for broader
development. Community participation in the construction of a health facility requires the community to
provide labor, to be partially remunerated. This is seen less as a matter of cost containment than as a way
to increase community ownership of the health facility. Moreover, the choice of labor-intensive
technologies will create employment and income (in both cash and food), thereby increasing food security
for the population and facilitating resettlement and re-integration. The projects will be implemented
through MOH-community-NGO partnerships. The Fund will be initially administered by the World Food
Program (WFP), though responsibilities for location determination, construction supervision and
maintenance will be shared between provincial and central health administrations. However, two provinces
will be pilots for direct provincial administration of the fund, immediately upon credit effectiveness. Later
in Program implementation, fund administration would be transferred to provincial governments with
proven capacity. It is presumed that the transfer of such administrative functions to the provinces will
be completed by the end of the third year of the Program life. (Para. 2.9)
3.7    The Directorate of Human Resources will be directly responsible for implementation of the
following components: (a) Health Manpower Development Plan Implementation; (b) Training Capacity
Improvement; and (c) Continuing Education. The component in support of the Faculty of Medicine will
be a responsibility of the Faculty of Medicine at the Eduardo Mondlane University.
3.8    All the formal training of health staff included in the Program will be carried out at the Health
Training Institutes and Health Training Centers, of which there is at least one in every province, under
contracts between the individual training institution and the Directorate of Human Resources. Other
general training, such as accounting, finance and computer operations, will be carried out by institutions
outside the Ministry.



-35-
Program Implementation Capacity
3.9    The MOH has overcome the implementation problems encountered in the first years of Health I.
The MOH is now prepared to implement successfully the proposed, much larger Program, thanks to
further actions based on lessons learned .n Health I, reduction in the size of the originally proposed credit,
reductions in the proposed Program's demands on MOH implementation capacity, much more thorough
preparation of components, a better-defined national health strategy and an improved implementation
framework, and greatly strengthened donor coordination and consensus in support of health sector
recovery. A significant proportion of the proposed credit and of the overall program will finance capacity
expansion and building, including the capacity to implement projects and to continue to rationalize and
focus the acquisition and use of donor resources in support of the health sector recovery program.
3.10   Application of Lessons Learned in Health I in the Design of the Proposed Program. Design of
the proposed Program has benefitted greatly from the application of lessons learned in Health I. At the
time of Health I's effectiveness, most of its components were at early stages of preparation, few terms
of reference for consultants and few procurement documents had been prepared, and much of the costing
was still tentative. The first project's delays in tendering have been countered by extensive planning and
preparation of tender documents during Program preparation. (Boxes 3 and 4). Recognition of the
importance of reliable and direct communication with the Bank led to the establishment of All-in-I
communications with the MOH, which are now being upgraded and expanded. Flexibility to reallocate
funds during annual reviews according to implementation performance will also help to improve
implementation and permit greater responsiveness to borrower needs and to the changing national
situation.
3.11  Some Features of the Proposed Program Enhancing MOH Implementation. Features incorporated
in the proposed Program to help assure effective implementation in specific areas can be summarized as
follows:
Civil Works: The Rural Health Development Fund is expected to be administered by the WFP
initially, in several provinces, with administrative responsibility shifting to the provincial level
MOH offices as their administrative capacity is sufficiently strengthened; actual execution of the
component will be carried out by NGOs. Rural hospitals, training institutes and warehouses will
all be procured out in the private sector, and complete tender documents have been prepared.
Additionally, a management contractor will be hired by GACOPI to assist with all civil works
implementation. These features will help avoid two major bottlenecks encountered in Health I,
weak administrative and civil works implementation capacity and slow preparation of tender
documents.
Equipment: Tender documents have been prepared, including most lists of equipment (a major
bottleneck early in Health I). A procurement agency will be hired to procure medical equipment.
Goods and Services:  Tender documents have been prepared.  The government-owned
procurement agency (MEDIMOC) has an excellent track record in procurement of
pharmaceuticals, laboratory reagents and medical supplies.

*      Trainin2: Tender documents have been prepared. The number of courses scheduled to be carried
out is about half of the number IDA was requested to finance on the basis of the original
projections of the Health Manpower Development Plan, having been reduced greatly during
Program preparation on the basis of experience in the first years of implementation of the plan.

B. Program Monitoring and Reporting
3.12   As the implementation of the Integrated Health Sector Program gradually shifts from a project
basis to an approach centered along activity lines, supervision processes will also be increasingly
undertaken along activity groupings. Supervision missions and supervision instruments (e.g., financial
audits) as well as procurement and disbursement practices and other common procedures will be organized
so as to minimize their demand on scarce implementation capacity in the MOH. In supervision and



-37-
monitoring the Program will benefit from the largely untapped potential for inter-donor delegation of
supervision of activities. This will permit a substantial reduction in the number of missions visiting the
sector and will help change the focus of assistance and government planning towards sector strategies
rather than segmented projects.
3.13   Monitoring procedures and Program reports will focus on progress in and impacts of the
implementation of the MOH's Program, measured against specific targets and actions set in the five year
rolling plans and detailed implementation programs, all of which will be reviewed and revised each year
throughout the Program life. Monitoring procedures and Program reports are designed to meet MOH
needs for information for decision-making, to fulfill the reporting requirements of donors, and to ensure
that the plans, programs and actions are in accordance with the MOH's strategy for the health sector.
General progress of the program will be measured against a list of key indicators (Annex-3.3). This
limited list of indicators focusses on some critical inputs (e.g., share of health sector in public budget,
disbursement level, number of facilities built), outputs (e.g., vaccination coverage, drugs availability),
and outcomes (infant, child and maternal mortality rates, user satisfaction) of the MOH program. Areas
of special focus, in keeping with the MOH's strategy, will include progress in assuring equitable
population access to health services providing at least a basic package of health services (with adequate
quality), coverage indicators, compliance of staffing with national norms, availability and effectiveness
of key inputs and support for health services, and progress in implementation of policies and programs
in areas such as infrastructure rehabilitation, human resources, and institutional strengthening.
3.14   Each year, the MOH will update the five year rolling plan and will prepare the investment and
recurrent budgets for the next year. These will be discussed with donors. The rolling plan and program
would identify and prioritize, in the context of the MOH health strategy, the areas for which donor
support is solicited. During the annual national consultations, IDA and cooperating donors would discuss
the proposed plans and investments in light of criteria that include cost-effectiveness, institutional capacity
(at central, provincial and district levels), cost recovery potential, and effectiveness, affordability,
accessibility and equity of services. An assessment of the effects of decentralizing authority during the
preceding year, and plans for the upcoming year, will also be on the agenda. In calendar year 1998, a
midterm review of the Program will be carried out .
3.15   The Ministry's departments and all donors to the sector will participate in annual review
workshops, to take place in April following the national consultations. During these workshops, Program
progress will be monitored and the Ministry will present a revised program of actions. Prior to the annual
workshop with donors the MOH will present a report on implementation and impact of the Health Sector
Program. The report will be distributed to the donors no later than March 1 of each year. The report will
discuss, inter alia, the progress in meeting the agreed output targets, a summary of the beneficiary
assessments and other external reviews of the previous year, with a summary of actions proposed and
taken to correct any problems found. Progress in the different components, issues hindering success, and
proposed solutions will be discussed and decided upon in the workshop. At the end of each workshop,
donor commitments to the various program components will be expressed and IDA funds may be
reallocated to finance gaps in identified priority areas.
3.16   The MOH will prepare semi-annual reports which will tabulate and graphically display physical
and financial progress during the previous semester and accumulated progress against agreed targets in
the Implementation Plan and Health Services Delivery Action Plan and highlight problems, with
recommendations for solving them. These reports will be distributed to donors within one month of the
end of each semester and will be the subject of a meeting of the Minister of Health's Advisory Council.



-38-
Minutes of the meeting will be attached to the next such report, with comments indicating actions taken
since the last meeting. A yearly report will summarize the status of each component and the degree of
compliance with donor covenants and agreements. The format of these reports will be designed to fulfill
the MOH needs for summary information for decision making and will also be adapted to satisfy IDA's
and other donors' reporting requirements.
3.17   Within six months of the closing of the IDA credit, the MOH will prepare a final report on the
implementation of the Program.
C. Procurement
3.18   Procurement under the project would be carried out in accordance with the Guidelines:
Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits of January, 1995 with emphasis on community
participation. The MOH, has prepared a draft procurement/disbursement plan for Bank review. This plan
will be the basis for project implementation and monitoring by GACOPI. The plan will be reviewed and
updated at least three months prior to the start of each Program year. GACOPI has agreed to use a
computerized project management software which includes procurement planning.
3.19   The Bank's Standard Bidding Documents (SBD) will be used for International Competitive
Bidding (ICB) procurements. Bidding documents agreed upon by the Bank and used in Health I will be
used for National Competitive Bidding (NCB) procurements under this Program. The Bank's Standard
Forms of Contract for Consultant Services will also be used where applicable. One ICB contract
document and one NCB document for procurement of civil works and goods have been reviewed and
approved by IDA during appraisal and negotiations. All of the above document formats are available in
computerized format.
3.20   Civil Works' total cost is US$ 27.6 million. All contracts for construction and rehabilitation of
hospitals, warehouses and training centers valued at US$ 500,000 or greater (US$ 15.8 million or 57.3
percent of total civil works value) shall be procured through ICB. Qualified local contractors participating
in ICB would be given a 7.5 percent margin of preference in accordance with the Guidelines. Similar
contracts valued at less than US$ 500,000 up to an aggregate total of US$ 600,000 will be procured using
NCB procedures approved in the Health I Project. Construction and rehabilitation of about 240 scattered
health posts plus 600 staff houses and 240 latrines and water wells aggregating to US$ 11.2 million, with
a maximum value of US$ 60,000 per contract, would be administered through the Rural Health
Development Fund with community participation in sub-project implementation. The MOH will contract
with NGOs or small local firms directly on the basis of standard unit prices fixed by the MOH, in
accordance with procedures acceptable to IDA. An estimated 80 percent of the cost of each health unit
will be financed by IDA and 20 percent by the World Food Program through a food for work scheme.
This approach will promote the utilization of local know-how and products and promote effective local
participation; these contracts are small in value and scattered in nature and would not attract established
national contractors. (paras. 2.9 and 3.6)



-39-

furniture equipment and supplies, training center furniture and supplies, warehouse stacking and
operational equipment, transport vehicles, pharmaceuticals, laboratory reagents and medical supplies,
textbooks and learning materials, and office furniture, equipment, technology supplies and fuel.
Procurements will be bulked where feasible and packages valued at US$ 100,000 or more will be
procured using ICB (US$ 35.0 million or 76.4 percent). Procurements valued at less than US$ 100,000
up to an aggregate total of US$ 2.4 million will be procured using NCB procedures agreed to in the
Health I Project. Implementation of the program would require the purchase of relatively small, primarily

local distributors. These purchases will be largely made by provincial governments through Simplified
Disbursement Procedures which provide for advancing of funds from the special account for a period of
up to three months to implement a clearly defined work program (paras. 3.28 and 3.30).
3.22   Consultants' services to an aggregate total of US$ 13.7 million for civil works Programn
supervision, human resources development, financial management, information systems development,
procurement agents and other supervision and administration functions will be selected in accordance the
Bank's Guidelines for Use of Consultants dated August, 1981. Terms of reference for all major
consultancy contracts have been reviewed and approved by IDA during appraisal and negotiations.
3.23   Training costs financed by IDA aggregating to US$ 16.5 million include fees for courses and
workshops for Program-related subjects. Recurrent costs for such items as travel tickets related to
training activities are included in this category along with fellowship allowances consisting of room, board
and living expense stipends to national students resident and non-resident at the MOH training centers for
long-term multi-year programs. All training costs will be based upon reasonable established rates
acceptable to IDA.
3.24   IDA Review  All contracts for construction of civil works and purchase of goods procured
following ICB procedures would be subject to prior review. The use of the Bank's SBD would
considerably expedite the prior review process as IDA review would primarily focus on invitation to bid,
bid data sheet, contract data, technical specifications, bill of quantities/schedule of requirements and other
contract-specific items. The first contracts for goods and works procured following NCB procedures
would also be subject to prior review by IDA. The provisions of the Consultant Guidelines requiring
prior IDA review or approval of budgets, short lists, selection procedures, letters of invitation, proposals,
evaluation reports and contracts shall not apply to contracts with firms estimated to cost less than US$
100,000 and with individuals estimated to cost less than US$ 50,000 each. However, this exception to
prior IDA review shall not apply to terms of reference, to single source selection of firms, to assignments
of a critical nature as reasonably determined by IDA or to amendments of contracts raising the contract
value to US$ 100,000 or above for contracts with firms and US$ 50,000 or above for contracts with
individuals. All procurements which are not subject to prior review by IDA however will be subject to
random post review by IDA during scheduled supervision missions. The MOH is required to maintain
all relevant procurement documentation for subsequent review by IDA. The MOH will submit to IDA
periodic procurement schedules detailing each procurement in progress and completed as part of the
normal project reporting exercise.
3.25   All imports valued at US$ 5,000 or more each and procured under this Program shall be subject
to preshipment inspection by the government's designated agents for quantity and for quality. If the
procurement was not made through ICB, preshipment inspection shall also include price verification.



-41-
3.26   All thresholds stated in this section shall be reviewed by the Borrower and IDA on an annual
basis. Modifications may be agreed upon based on performance and actual values of procurements
implemented. Amendments to the Credit agreement may be prepared as necessary.

E. Program Accounts and Audits
Program Accounts
3.29   Program accounts will be established and maintained by the MOH. A Special Account will be
opened for this credit and maintained with a conrnercial bank, acceptable to IDA. The maximum balance
in the Special Account will be US$ 6.5 million, which will cover about 4 months of expenditures.
3.30   Satisfactory accounting systems have been maintained by the Program management unit (GACOPI)
under the First Health Project. IDA funds will flow into the Program in three different ways, to be
accounted for as follows:
(a)    Civil Works under the Rural Health Development Fund: Under this component,
sub-projects costing not more than US$ 60,000 each will be financed for Provincial
Directorates of Health (PDH) under the implementation of a non-governmental
organization (NGO) approved by the PDH and WFP. At the end of each semester, WFP
will present to GACOPI an account, supported by invoices of the expenditures. GACOPI
will request IDA to effect a subsequent disbursement in accordance with the WFP
activities planned for the following semester and, in turn, present to IDA (semi-annually)
a replenishment request under the SOE procedure. Over the life of the Program, GACOPI
is expected to finance on average US$ 1 million in civil works per province (i.e. about
US$ 10 million for the whole country) by this method.



-43-
(b)    Operating Costs and Consumables under General Province Operations (GPOs): IDA is
expected to provide financing of about US$ 100,000 per province per year, to support the
general operations of the Provincial and District Directorates of Health following the
Simplified Disbursement Procedures described in para. 3.28 above. Although these funds
will support the general health budget, they will not be provided as an integral part of the
government budget, but parallel to it. This is to prevent them from suffering the usual
fate of government budgetary allocations, which get lost to the recipient if they are not
spent by the end of the year.
(c)    All other funds: will be accounted for by GACOPI in the same way as the IDA funds
used in the First Health Project.
Audits
3.31   The audit of a Program raises the issue of whether the external auditor should audit all the
expenditures of the Ministry - an unusually expensive and mammoth task for an international accounting
firm - or audit only the use of IDA funds. In this Program it has been decided to limit the external audit
by an independent auditor to the use of IDA funds.
3.32   The Borrower will employ an independent auditor acceptable to IDA to audit the use of all IDA
funds applied in financing the Program. The only unusual aspect of the audit concerns the IDA funds to
be disbursed for GPOs. These particular funds are audited by the Ministry of Finance whose audit is
independently checked annually by auditors of the SDC. To avoid excessive duplication, the SDC has
agreed to coordinate the annual audits of GPOs.
3.33   The independent auditor to be employed by the Borrower to audit the use of IDA funding for the
Program will be required to express a separate opinion in the same audit report on: (a) the Program
accounts, showing all IDA financing for the Program; (b) the Special Account operated by the Program;
and (c) the Statements of Expenditures, including those used for civil works under the Rural Health
Development Fund.
3.34   As soon as possible after the end of the financial year, but no later than nine months after its
completion, the government will present audits of the special account and the Program accounts. The
costs of the audits are eligible for financing out of the proceeds of the credit.
3.35   The auditor's terms of reference reflecting the conditions described above were reviewed and
agreed by IDA during negotiations. An accounting manual will be prepared and submitted to IDA for
review prior to credit signature.



-44-
IV. BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. Benefits
4.1    The main benefits of the Program will be increased coverage and quality of health services and
increased equity in access to services with a shift of resources toward rural areas and a basic package of
health care. The main beneficiaries of improved basic facilities will be women and children as the primary
users of first level health care through MCH-related programs. MOH staff morale and performance are
expected to benefit from improved training, working conditions and incentives to which the Program
would contribute. Efficiency of the health services system is also expected to improve with increased skills
and the shift of human resources toward rural areas, the base levels of the system, and the relatively
under-served Northern provinces. The Program will generate important external benefits by assisting the
MOH in the coordination and implementation of projects undertaken by all donors, within the overall
Program. The Program will also assist in increasing the transparency and efficiency of the sector's
budgetary process by moving a significant portion of the off-budget expenditures into the budget. (paras
2.1-2.2)
B. Risks
4.2    The main risk of the Program is the MOH's potential difficulty to fully implement parts of the
sector's extensive list of activities, leading to bottlenecks and inefficient service provision. To address this
risk, the Program has been designed to support the improvement of management capacity in the MOH
and aims to coordinate donors' activities so that implementation constraints are alleviated. Moreover, the
Program's concentration of financing in non-civil works is an attempt to reduce pressure in the area of
infrastructure implementation, which has proved to be one of the main obstacles to the satisfactory
implementation of the Health and Nutrition Project. As for human resources development, the MOH has
limited capacity to manage training resources. To mitigate this problem, training activities and capacity
improvements will continue to be carefully timed and outputs modestly estimated based on recent past
experience. Finally, experience from the Health and Nutrition Project has also shown tender document
preparation to be one of the most stringent constraints to the successful implementation of a health project
in Mozambique. In order to ensure Program implementation success, from the start, the MOH has
undertaken an unprecedented effort to prepare tender documents for all major procurement packages prior
to appraisal. (paras. 3.9-3.11)
C. Environmental Considerations
4.3    The Program has been determined to be in Category C. The main positive environmental impact
the Program is expected to achieve relates to the development of medical waste disposal guidelines to be
adopted in every health unit constructed by the Program, with financing for an evaluation of the status
of hospital waste disposal provided for in the Policy Development component. Moreover, proper sanitary
conditions will be introduced in every health unit and solar energy technology in every rural health
center/post financed by IDA. Since most health units built by the Program consist of small structures,
and since most civil works in large structures involve rehabilitation rather than expansion, negative
enviromnental outputs due to construction are expected to be negligible. No environmental analysis has
been prepared for this Program. (para 5.1 (c))



-45-
D. Economic Analysis
4.4    The project is designed to achieve high gains in health status with the available resources.
Because the project contains a diverse set of components to provide improved management, logistical
efficiency, and rehabilitation of facilities, as well as components directly increasing health status, it is not
possible to obtain precise estimates of the economic benefits.  The sector strategy, on which the
management, logistics and rehabilitation components are focused, however, is the delivery of an
appropriate basic package of services. An economic analysis of the contents of the basic package
demonstrated that they are appropriate to the burden of disease in Mozambique and are expected to be
cost effective in reducing the burden.
4.5    Ranked in descending order of the percent of total years of life lost from premature death, the
major eight disease categories are malaria (16.6 percent), acute respiratory diseases (14.6), diarrheal
disease (14.3), childhood immunizable diseases (12.7), perinatal causes (8.0), injuries (7.8),
cardiovascular (5.3), tuberculosis (4.6), sexually transmitted diseases (3.3), nutritional deficiencies (2.5).
Correspondingly, the basic package supported by the project would provide cost effective treatment
interventions to address malaria, acute respiratory disease, diarrhea, delivery and postpartum care (which
can reduce perinatal causes), trauma, malnutrition, and preventive interventions to reduce the incidence
of childhood inmmunizable diseases, health education and family planning services to reduce STDs, and
nutritional monitoring. Annex-3.7 provides a comparison of the cost effectiveness of the basic treatment
and preventive activities included in the basic package with less cost effective alternatives.
4.6    With only a few exceptions, the most cost effective activities (those below US$ 2.00 per year of
life gained'6) are community based or primary health care interventions. Only a few secondary and
tertiary care interventions are below US$ 3.00 per year of life gained. The most cost effective application
of secondary and tertiary care is the treatment of acute respiratory infections and diarrhea. Treatment of
injuries and chronic diseases of adults is much less cost effective. Because the project contains wide
latitude in implementation of components, the cost effectiveness analysis will be continued during
implementation and discussed with the MOH to promote cost effective adjustment of individual activities.
4.7    In addition to the focus on cost effective interventions, the Program is expected to yield substantial
reductions in cost per life year saved. For example, the combined impact of increased coverage and
improved quality of services (including better sector management) is estimated to reduce the cost per life
year saved for treatment of children with respiratory diseases, malaria and diarrhea diseases by about
55 %. Even if the rate of implementation of the infrastructure expansion program is 50% slower than
predicted, the cost per life year saved is still reduced by nearly 20%.
16 This is the cost per life year saved for those treated. Since coverage is sparse, the aggregate cost per life year saved by
the treatment of these diseases (i.e., taking into account difficulty of access to a health unit by the population) is higher (see Annex
3.7 Tables E and F).
