Dear Sir,
Re: Mozambicue Health Sector Letter of Policv
Introduction
Mozambique's epidemiological pattern and its health indicators are typical of a
developing country in a pre-transitional period. Available data on morbidity and
mortality indicate infectious and parasitic diseases as the main causes of illness and
death; namely malaria, acute gastrointestinal and respiratory infections, measles,
tuberculosis and parasitic infections of the digestive system and urinary tract.
Quantitative and qualitative dietary deficiencies,  inadequate hygiene and
environment sanitation, safe water shortages, limited access to health care,
illiteracy and insufficient access to risk-reduction information are all compounding
factors in morbidity and mortality, especially for women and children. High
maternal and infant mortality rates have been associated with insuffcient health
care provision. More recently, anecdotal observations suggest that violence and
road accidents are among the major causes of death among adults, particularly in
urban settings. Of the total hospital deaths among adults in the country,
tuberculosis accounts for about 29%. The HIV pandemic in the c6lntry presents the
same trend of other African countries; i.e., heterosexual transmission and geometric
evolution of the incidence rate. By 1994 about 1,360 AIDS cases have been reported
to WHO; but the real number is thought to be higher given the diagnostic
constraints related to lack of laboratory facilities and limited access to health care.
According to surveys carried out by the Ministry of Health (MoH), the prevalence
of chronic malnutrition (height-for age) is around 30-40% while acute malnutrition



-50 -                               Annex -1.2 A
Page 2 of 11
prevalence (weight-for-height) is estimated in 6% of those surveyed. Despite this,
recent data suggests a slight improvement in nutritional status in some parts of the
country during 1993 as a result of peace and resumption of farming and economic
activities in the countryside. However, 60% of the population are living below the
poverty line making the majority of the population a "vulnerable group". In general,
nutritional problems are still prevalent and may exasperate existing health
problems. In addition, the drought that affects the central and southern part of the
country poses a threat to the food security of a considerable number of persons.
Average life expectancy, estimated from the 1980 census data, ranges from 42 to
45 years. Infant mortality rate was estimated to be 162 per 1,000 live birth, and
child mortality rate stands at 275 per 1,000. In general, Mozambique's social
indicators are lower than those of Sub-Saharan Africa.
Following the establishment of peace and security, refugees and displaced persons
begun to return to their places of origin or choice. Demand for health care is
increasing in urban settings and places of resettlement of former displaced persons.
Health Policy and Strategy: the future National Health System (NHS)
The basic assumptions underlying the strategy for the NHS reconstruction and
expansion, and the vision sketched below, are the following:
* peace and political stability
* the absence of major natural disasters
* a sustained economic growth
* the government's unchanging commitment to Health
* continuous support from the international community
* a progressive reduction of poverty, enabling more people to contribute to
service financing.
The transition from emergency relief to rehabilitation and development imposes
changes both in policy and in approaches. The reconstruction of the Health sector is
not the simple restoration of physical infrastructures destroyed during the war. It
offers opportunities for redefining development priorities and readjusting some
major imbalances in resource allocation, planning the health network according to
the present and future demand and attempting to redress the imbalances inherited
from the colonial rule.
The reconstruction strategy encompasses: (i) maximizing the benefits from the use
of available resources, (ii) fairness in distributing resources and equity in the
access to health care, by region and social groups; (iii) user's free choice of health
care provider; (iv) integration of the public and private sectors (including NGOs);
(v) preserving the positive experience gained by the NHS in implementing primary
health care.
Health care and Rehabilitation Strategy
Comprehensive Primary Health Care will be the basis for health care delivery;
services' coverage will be expanded in rural areas. Neglected provinces, such as
Zamb6zia, will get substantial extra investments, to reduce the existing gap.



Annex - 1.2A
-51 -                                Page 3 of 11
Assurance of timely referral of patients to higher tiers of the health care system
constitutes an important concern. . Almost all existing health units, from health
posts to hospitals, need renovation or rebuilding and improvements in their
supportive diagnostic facilities. Similarly, improved management, efficiency and
quality of hospitals, as well as better facilities and equipment maintenance are
considered crucial.
The MoH will gradually expand the existing health network with emphasis on the
PHC level. The Governrent will encourage the participation of non-profit, non-
government organizations, both national and international, in the rehabilitation
process, addressing the need for readjusting the present imbalance in the network
distribution, locating facilities near population settlements, or where users can
easily reach them by road.
The MoH projections predict the introduction of the Small Health Center (Centro de
Sauide tipo III), besides health centers and health posts. The Small Health Center
is a unit capable of providing preventive and MCH & FP services, along with
outpatients and medical emergencies. Most os these units will be the result of the
upgrading of the existing health posts. By the year 2005, there should be 1,284
first level health units, i.e., 173 Large Health Centers (Centro de Sauide tipo I &
II), 661 Small Health Centers and 450 Health Posts.
Immunization delivery points will double, up to 850 and vaccinations will be
offered routinely at the health facilities and outside, reaching remote communities.
Clinical laboratories will function in each "Large" Health Center. MCH care will be
systematically included in the package. A substantial improvement in the people's
health status is expected from the implementation of this strategy.
Rural hospitals play a crucial role in the health care delivery system, providing
referral and logistic support to the first level; they enhance the system credibility,
making critical services available in rural areas. Concentrating better (to some
extent) resources available in rural areas, represents a model of health care and an
attraction pole for users. Unfortunately, mainly because of capital shortage, the
rural hospitals' network has always been insufficient in Mozambique. Its extension
is considered an essential move for the whole reconstruction exercise. In this view,
all the existing 24 rural hospitals should be rehabilitated by the year 2005. Many of
them will be upgraded and other 15 rural hospitals will be built in the same period.
Most of the new proposed rural hospitals should result from upgrading and
expansion of existing health centers. The selection criteria for its location include
the size of the target population, availability of adequate communication (roads)
and the economic importance of the district. Once the cycle is completed, an
average rural hospital will have a hundred beds, providing referral and backup
services (drug and vaccine distribution, supervision, etc.) to a cluster of 3-4
districts, serving a population of 300,000-500,000. This will be well below African
standards (100,000-200,000), but a dramatic progress for rural Mozambique.
Top-level facilities include Provincial,  Central and Specialized Hospitals,  all
located in urban areas, which also have a comparatively privileged primary health
care network. Given health expenditure constraints, no significant expansion is
expected in the urban areas. The hospitals absorb most of the Health sector
expenditures. The rehabilitation strategy encompasses the physical rehabilitation
XtD



Annex - 1.2A
Page 4 of 11
- 52 -
of these health units that have had no maintenance during the past two decades,
and organizational steps toward better service quality and efficiency.
Decentralization
Local authorities will become totally or partially responsible for several activities:
-management of some local revenues (fees, etc.)
-small scale investments
-management of special program's funds
-facilities and equipment maintenance
-in-service training
-elementary-level pre-service training
-basic-and elementary staff management, including hiring and dismissing
-community health workers' training, support, supply, and supervision
MoH' headquarters will be still in charge of critical activities:
-deployment of university-and mid-level personnel
-import of drugs
-large investments
-pre-service training
-allocation of public funds
-policy making and strategic planning
-standards setting
-quality control
Special health programs should be progressively reduced in size; some dismantled,
and others merged. Management of funds should devolve almost entirely to
provinces and later to districts. At this level, decisions should be taken together by
locally elected bodies and centrally appointed technical authorities.
The pace and the exact extent of these changes are unclear. The Government is still
exploring the issue. A progressive shift, made through small adjustments, is
anticipated. By 2002, some of the functions to be handed over to local authorities
might still be run by MoH. However, there is evidence that the thrust toward
decentralization is growing.
Human Resources
In developing human resources the aim is to improve the work-force's technical
skills, making sure that new employees are hired only when there is a
demonstrated need that cannot be met effectively by other means. Better staff
management at all levels should increase morale, productivity, and the equity and
quality of care. Better living conditions for health workers are instrumental to
stabilize and motivate them.
In the short run, the number of health workers should not increase, but rather be
kept at the present figure of approximately 17,000. This, coupled with an increase
in state revenues, should allow an improvement in staff earnings in real terms.
The strategy and priorities set by the government permit a proportional increase of
skilled personnel in relation to unskilled staff, from the 1991 ratio of 1:1 to 2:1 by
the year 2002. The number of mid-level professionals should increase through the
At



Annex - 1.2A
Page 5 of 11
- 53 -
replacement of losses with more qualified staff and through promotion from lower
levels. The Health Manpower Development Plan (HMDP) gives priority to
training PHC-oriented personnel. Substantial inputs will be given for the training
of Medical Assistants (Tecnicos de Medicina) and Medical Agents (Agentes de
Medicina) who are expected to be in charge of health centers, to lead health teams,
and at the same time provide curative care. By 2002, 1,500 Medical Assistants
would have been trained. At this point, large and small health centers will have at
least one T6cnico or Agente, respectively. They will run a facility adequately
staffed by a balanced health team: MCH, preventive medicine, inpatient care,
pharmacy, laboratory and administration will all be covered.
Staffing improvement will not be restricted to the PHC network; mid-level nurses
and midwives are being trained to strengthen hospital capacity, to cope with the
expected workload expansion and to improve the quality of care.
Financial Resources
The policies and strategies above outlined were translated in a sectoral program
covering the 1996-2000 period: the Health Sector Recovery Program (HSRP), and
in the Economic and Social Program of the Government for the next five years
recently approved by the parliament. Financial resources for the HSRP will arise
from a number of different sources, i.e.; government, grant aid, and soft loan funds.
The IDA financed portion of the HSRP is one of the major loan sources and
constitutes a slice of the program financing during the period.
The government financial resources for the program were estimated from the
figures for 1995, a year in which a substantial increase in real terms (about 40%)
of the Health sector recurrent budget took place. During the program lifetime the
government will increase the sector's recurrent budget by 4 % p.a. in real terms. The
anticipated growth of the government financing means an increase in the per-capita
recurrent expenditure of approximately 1.3% p.a., and will allow for improvement of
the quality of services delivered to the population.
Based on GDP growth estimates of 5% and Recurrent Public Expenditure growth of
1.8% p.a. (payment of interests excluded) the share of the Health sector in the
recurrent budget will increase from 6.2% in 1995 to 6.9% in the year 2000. It is also
anticipated that the Public Investment Budget  (government contribution to
externally financed projects) will increase at a rate of 3% p.a. during the program
life while the Health sector investment budget will grow 4 % p.a., in the same
period. Therefore, the share of the Health sector in the investment budget will
increase from 6.9% in 1995 to about 7.2% by the year 2000. These increases in the
recurrent and investment budgets will be revised annually to reflect improvements
in macro-economic performance.
Cost Recovery
Cost recovery,  excluding drugs,  represented about 4.2%  of Government
recurrent health expenditures in 1987. By 1995 cost sharing represented only 2% of
the government recurrent expenditure. A plan will be operationalized to implement
the recommendations of a cost recovery study conducted under the IDA financed
Health and Nutrition Project. The plan includes among other measures updating of
IL



Annex - 1.2A
-54 -
Page 6 of 11
the fees that are collected in the health units, both in-patients and outpatients
charges, reduction of government subsidy for pharmaceuticals dispensed in the
public sector, regulation of the "special clinics", and other internal administrative
measures to enhance revenue generation and utilization. It is anticipated that the
level of cost recovery will increase from 2 % to 10 % of the government recurrent
expenditure in the 1996-2000 period. This will help to ensure a long term stability
of internally generated financial resources for the sector. Measures will be taken to
safeguard the accessibility of the poor people to health care.
The distribution of the financial resources along the Program lifetime is shown
below: the figures are in millions USD, at the estimated average exchange rate for
1995.
1996  1997  1998  1999   2000
l.Counterpart funds for investment   4.1   4.3   4.4   4.6      4.8
2. GoM Budget                      16.1  16.7  17.4  18.1    18.8
3. Cost recovery                    0.8   1.3   1.3   1.8        1.9
Total  21.0  22.3  23.2  24.5    25.5
Finally, the ratio of Public Health Expenditures to the GDP will (with cost recovery
included) will be at least maintained at the present level of 1.5% of GDP in each
year of the period 1996-2000.
Maputo, September 1995
Luisa Dias Diogo
(Deputy Minister of Planning and Finance)

MOZAMBIOUE Health Sector Recovery Project (HRSP): Letter of
Commitment of Local Resources
1.   This letter is to confirm that the Government of Mozambique
(GOM) is committed to implement a five year $ 355 million USD
multi-donor financed program: The Health Sector Recovery
Program.
2.   The GOM is committed to include in the Trianual Investment
Plan (PTIP) the necessary counterpart funds for the programed
investment.  The  GOM  will  finance  the  "required  local
contribution"i for the various components of the program, as
agreed  with   the   concerned   financing   agencies.   This
contribution will include both investment counterpart and
domestic recurrent expenditures funds for the HSRP.
3.   The  GOM  financial  resources  for  the  program  were
estimated from the figures for 1995, a year in which a
substantial increase in real terms   (about 40%) of the
Health sector recurrent budget took place. During the
program lifetime the government will increase the
sector's recurrent budget at minimum by 4 k p.a. in real
terms.
4.   The anticipated growth of the government financing means
an increase in the per-capita recurrent expenditure of
approximately 1.3% p.a., and will allow for a significant



Annex - 1.2B
-61-                              Page 2 of2
improvement of the quality of services delivered to the
population.
S.   It is also anticipated that the government contribution to
Investment Budget will increase at a rate of 3% p.a. during
the program life while the Health sector Investment Budget
will grow at 4% p.a. in the same period.
6.   The GOM is committed to revise annually the above stated
increases in the rate of growth in recurrent and investment
expenditures of the Health Sector in a manner commensurate
with    improvements    in    macro-economic    perfomance.
7.   A cost recovery plan will be operationalized to implement the
recommendations of a study conducted under the IDA financed
Health and Nutrition Project. The plan includes, among other
measures, updating of the fees that are collected in the
health units, both in-patients and outpatients charges,
reduction of government subsidy for pharmaceuticals dispensed
in the public sector,  regulation of the 'special clinics',
and other internal administrative measures to enhance revenue
generation and utilization. It is anticipated that the level
of cost recovery will increase from 2 % to 10 I of the
government recurrent expenditure in the 1996-2000 period.
This will help to ensure a long term stability of internally
generated financial resources for the sector. Measures will
be taken to safeguard the accessibility of the poor people to
health care.
sa Dias D' go
(Vice-Minister of Planning aid Finance)

Republic of Mozambique
Health Sector Recovery Program
OUTLINE OF IMPLEMENTATION MANUAL
PART A:       RESUMPTION OF AND IMPROVEMENT IN THE QUALITY OF HEALTH SERVICES
1. Rehabilitation and expansion of the network of rural/general hospitals, and rehabilitation of the network of
provincial and central hospitals
*      MOH Project Document
*      The particular tender documents and contracts for the civil works in Buzi, Mueda, Gurue and Nacala
*      Contract for supervision of the civil works.
*      The standard lists of equipment for second level health facilities.
*      Tender documents and contracts for equipment for the facilities.
2. Rehabilitation and expansion of the network of first level health facilities (HDF)
e      MOH Project Document
*      An implementation manual defining the role of each partner in the fund regarding management,
administration and implementation.
*      Civil works manual, defining standards for construction.
*      The standard lists of equipment for first level health facilities.
*      Standard contract for the supervision of the works.
3. Strengthening laboratory facilities in rural/provincial hospitals and health centers
*      MOH Project document with annexes.
e      Contract with a firm to assess the rehabilitation in the prioritized facilities.
*      Tender documents for the priority rehabilitation of laboratory facilities.
*      Standard lists of lab equipment for each level of facility.
4. Stability in the provision of medical supplies, pharmaceuticals and laboratory reagents.
*      The Bank's standard procurement document for pharmaceuticals and vaccines.
5. Support for a national maintenance strategy.
*      MOH Project Document
*      The Bank's standard tender documents for goods.
6. Support for institutional nutrition program.
*      WFP Project document



Annex - 3.2
-92-                                     Page 2 of 2
PART B:        IMPROVEMENT OF SECTOR INSTITUTIONS, SUPPORT SERVICES
AND SUB-NATIONAL HEALTH MANAGEMENT SYSTEMS
1. Establishment of policies and procedures and expanding storage capacity for the supply system for medical
supplies and pharmaceuticals
MOH Project Document
*      The particular tender documents and contracts for the civil works in Pemba, Lichinga, Matola and
Beira.
*      Contract for supervision of the civil works.
*      The lists of equipment for the warehouses.
*      Tender docs and contracts for equipment.
2. Improvement of the provincial health management system
*      MOH Project Document
3. Strengthening the sector's capacity to implement its programs
-      MOH Project Document

MOZAMBIQUE
HEALTH SECTOR RECOVERY PROGRAM
SUPERVISION PLAN
Supervision of the Program will be arranged and timed in such a way that the effort is most useful in
the program cycle of the Ministry of Health, immediately after the national consultations in the
Conselho Coordinador in March/April of each year and again six months later. It is envisioned that
considerable staffing resources will be required for the supervision of the Program, especially if
increased donor coordination is to be achieved over the first years of the Program. However, a
reduction of the Bank's inputs into supervision should be possible at a later stage, as the implementation
of the integrated sector program, supported by all donors, gradually shifts from a project basis to an
approach along activity groupings, with supervision tasks divided accordingly among donors.
Arrangements for joint supervision with other donors have been discussed during preparation. With
SDC, agreement has been reached for joint auditing of provincial operational budgets, which are
supported by both IDA and SDC. Joint supervision missions will reduce substantial the heavy burden
on the staff of the Ministry to attend to all supervising donors separately and will help change the focus
of assistance and Government planning from segmented projects to sector-wide programming.
The annual program implementation review, each March/April, will focus on overall achievements of
the program of the Ministry of Health in reaching set targets for outputs and outcomes. The review
will rely primarily on reports which the MOH itself needs as information for decision making. A list of
indicators to measure key inputs, outputs and outcomes has been developed by the - MOH and the
donors. Based on findings during the annual review, Program funds may be rescheduled within and
among components, as the Ministry updates its five year rolling plan.

Republic of Mozambique
Health Sector Recovery Program
Economic Analysis of the Basic Package
An analysis of the basic package was carried out in two steps. First, the basis for the package was
established through calculation of the burden of disease by individual categories. Second, the cost
effectiveness of activities in the basic package to reduce the burden of disease was established through a
cost effectiveness analysis across a number of alternative interventions.
Burden of Disease
Methodology. The years of life lost from premature death were calculated using age-specific mortality
rates by disease and a life table giving life expectancy by age cohort. The age-specific mortality pattern
for Sub-Saharan Africa used in the World Bank Global Burden of Disease (GBD) exercise was adapted
to Mozambique. Key inputs that were tailored to Mozambique were the mortality from HIV
seroprevalence, maternity, malaria nutritional deficiencies, and injuries, in addition to fertility, and the
population age structure.
Results. Table 3.7A ranks the major disease categories in decreasing order of importance. The top five
categories are malaria, acute respiratory infections (ARI), diarrhea, childhood diseases, and perinatal
mortality. Together these five causes account for over 65 percent of premature mortality. Aggregated
across major disease groups, communicable diseases account for 77 percent, chronic diseases 15 percent,
and injuries for 8 percent of premature mortality. Table 3.7B gives the detailed estimates of burden of
disease by age group and sex. The pattern is consistent with the level of development and recent social
stress in Mozambique. The pattern is also consistent with the project emphasis on a basic package of
primary services.
Cost Effectiveness
Methodology. The effects of selected interventions were measured in terms of life years saved and were
calculated from estimates of effectiveness, covered population, and the burden of disease. The estimates
of effects were adapted from the literature and ongoing studies for Tanzania and the Republic of Guinea.
Intervention costs were estimated from project information as well as adaptation of previous studies.
Costs were estimated from separate line items for overhead (administration and facility costs), wages,
equipment, drugs and supplies. Detailed tables setting out the assumptions and calculations for effects
and costs are available in the project files and are summarized in Tables 3.7C through 3.7F.
Results. The last column of Tables 3.7C and E give the cost per life years saved. The table gives the
cost effectiveness of 46 alternative interventions grouped according to the top ten causes of premature
mortality. The diseases for which the most cost effective interventions exist are ARI, malaria (with the
exception of treatment for severe malaria), diarrheal disease, childhood diseases (immunizations), and
selected activities for perinatal/maternal causes and nutrition. Within each category the lowest cost
interventions are usually delivered at the lowest levels of care. This is emphasized by Tables 3.7D and F
which provide a regrouping of the cost effectiveness estimates by level of care.
